sir never poor wight of a dedicator have less hope from his dedication than i have from this of mine for it be write in a bye corner of the kingdom and in a retir d thatch d house where i live in a constant endeavour to fence against the infirmity of ill health and other evil of life by mirth be firmly persuade that every time a man smiles but much more so when he laugh it add something to this fragment of life

the life and opinion of tristram shandy gent
chapter 1 i
i wish either my father or my mother or indeed both of they as they be in duty both equally bind to it have mind what they be about when they begot i have they duly consider d how much depend upon what they be then doing that not only the production of a rational being be concern in it but that possibly the happy formation and temperature of his body perhaps his genius and the very cast of his mind and for aught they know to the contrary even the fortune of his whole house might take their turn from the humour and disposition which be then uppermost had they duly weigh and consider all this and proceed accordingly i be verily persuade i should have make a quite different figure in the world from that in which the reader be likely to see me believe i good folk this be not so inconsiderable a thing as many of you may think it you have all i dare say hear of the animal spirit as how they be transfuse from father to son c
c and a great deal to that purpose well you may take my word that nine part in ten of a man s sense or his nonsense his success and miscarriage in this world depend upon their motion and activity and the different track and train you put they into so that when they be once set a-go whether right or wrong tis not a half- penny matter away they go clutter like hey-go mad and by tread the same step over and over again they presently make a road of it as plain and as smooth as a garden-walk which when they be once use to the devil himself sometimes shall not be able to drive they off it
pray my dear quoth my mother have you not forget to wind up the clock good g

chapter 1 ii
then positively there be nothing in the question that i can see either good or bad then let i tell you sir it be a very unseasonable question at least because it scatter and disperse the animal spirit whose business it be to have escort and go hand in hand with the homunculus and conduct he safe to the place destine for his reception
the homunculus sir in however low and ludicrous a light he may appear in this age of levity to the eye of folly or prejudice to the eye of reason in scientific research he stand confess d--a be guard and circumscribe with rights the minute philosopher who by the bye have the most enlarged understanding their soul be inversely as their enquiry shew we incontestably that the homunculus be create by the same hand engender d in the same course of nature endow d with the same loco-motive power and faculty with us that he consist as we do of skin hair fat flesh vein artery ligament nerve cartilage bone marrow brain gland genital humour and articulations is a being of as much activity and in all sens of the word as much and as truly our fellow-creature as my lord chancellor of england he may be benefitted he may be injured he may obtain redress in a word he have all the claim and right of humanity which tully puffendorf or the good ethick writer allow to arise out of that state and relation
now dear sir what if any accident have befall he in his way alone or that through terror of it natural to so young a traveller my little gentleman have get to his journey s end miserably spent his muscular strength and virility wear down to a thread his own animal spirit ruffle beyond description and that in this sad disorder d state of nerve he have lain down a prey to sudden start or a series of melancholy dream and fancy for nine long long month together i tremble to think what a foundation have be lay for a thousand weakness both of body and mind which no skill of the physician or the philosopher could ever afterwards have set thoroughly to right

to my uncle mr
toby shandy do i stand indebted for the precede anecdote to whom my father who be an excellent natural philosopher and much give to close reasoning upon the small matter have oft and heavily complain of the injury but once more particularly as my uncle toby well remember d upon his observe a most unaccountable obliquity as he call d it in my manner of set up my top and justify the principle upon which i have do it the old gentleman shake his head and in a tone more expressive by half of sorrow than reproach he say his heart all along forebode and he see it verify in this and from a thousand other observation he have make upon i that i should neither think nor act like any other man s child but alas

chapter 1 iv
i know there be reader in the world as well as many other good people in it who be no reader at all who find themselves ill at ease unless they be let into the whole secret from first to last of every thing which concern you
it be in pure compliance with this humour of theirs and from a backwardness in my nature to disappoint any one soul live that i have be so very particular already
as my life and opinion be likely to make some noise in the world and if i conjecture right will take in all rank profession and denomination of man whatever be no less read than the pilgrim s progress itself--and in the end prove the very thing which montaigne dread his essay should turn out that is a book for a parlour-window i find it necessary to consult every one a little in his turn and therefore must beg pardon for go on a little far in the same way for which cause right glad i be that i have begin the history of myself in the way i have do and that i be able to go on trace every thing in it as horace say ab ovo
horace i know do not recommend this fashion altogether but that gentleman be speak only of an epic poem or a tragedy i forget which besides if it be not so i should beg mr
horace s pardon for in writing what i have set about i shall confine myself neither to his rule nor to any man s rule that ever live
to such however as do not choose to go so far back into these thing i can give no well advice than that they skip over the remain part of this chapter for i declare before-hand tis write only for the curious and inquisitive
shut the door i be begot in the night betwixt the first sunday and the first monday in the month of march in the year of our lord one thousand seven hundred and eighteen
i be positive i was but how i come to be so very particular in my account of a thing which happen before i be bear be owe to another small anecdote know only in our own family but now make publick for the well clear up this point
my father you must know who be originally a turkey merchant but have leave off business for some year in order to retire to and die upon his paternal estate in the county of be i believe one of the most regular man in every thing he do whether twa matter of business or matter of amusement that ever live
as a small speciman of this extreme exactness of his to which he be in truth a slave he have make it a rule for many year of his life on the first sunday-night of every month throughout the whole year as certain as ever the sunday-night came to wind up a large house-clock which we have stand on the back-stair head with his own hands and be somewhere between fifty and sixty year of age at the time i have be speak of he have likewise gradually bring some other little family concernment to the same period in order as he would often say to my uncle toby to get they all out of the way at one time and be no more plague and pester with they the rest of the month
it be attend but with one misfortune which in a great measure fall upon myself and the effect of which i fear i shall carry with i to my grave namely that from an unhappy association of idea which have no connection in nature it so fall out at length that my poor mother could never hear the say clock wound up but the thought of some other thing unavoidably pop into her head-- vice versa which strange combination of idea the sagacious locke who certainly understand the nature of these thing well than most man affirm to have produce more wry action than all other source of prejudice whatsoever
but this by the bye
now it appear by a memorandum in my father s pocket-book which now lie upon the table that on lady-day which be on the 25 th of the same month in which i date my geniture my father set upon his journey to london with my eld brother bobby to fix he at westminster school and as it appear from the same authority that he do not get down to his wife and family till the second week in may following it bring the thing almost to a certainty
however what follow in the beginning of the next chapter put it beyond all possibility of a doubt
but pray sir what be your father do all december january and february why madam he be all that time afflict with a sciatica
chapter 1 v
on the fifth day of november 1718 which to the aera fix on be as near nine kalendar month as any husband could in reason have expected wa i tristram shandy gentleman bring forth into this scurvy and disastrous world of ours i wish i have be bear in the moon or in any of the planet except jupiter or saturn because i never could bear cold weather for it could not well have fare bad with i in any of they though i will not answer for venus than it have in this vile dirty planet of ours which o my conscience with reverence be it speak i take to be make up of the shred and clipping of the rest not but the planet be well enough provide a man could be bear in it to a great title or to a great estate or could any how contrive to be call up to public charge and employment of dignity or power but that be not my case and therefore every man will speak of the fair as his own market have go in it for which cause i affirm it over again to be one of the vile world that ever be made for i can truly say that from the first hour i draw my breath in it to this that i can now scarce draw it at all for an asthma i get in scat against the wind in flanders i have be the continual sport of what the world call fortune and though i will not wrong she by say she have ever make i feel the weight of any great or signal evil yet with all the good temper in the world i affirm it of she that in every stage of my life and at every turn and corner where she could get fairly at i the ungracious duchess have pelt i with a set of as pitiful misadventure and cross accident as ever small hero sustain
chapter 1 vi
in the beginning of the last chapter i inform you exactly when i be bear but i do not inform you how
no that particular be reserve entirely for a chapter by itself beside sir as you and i be in a manner perfect stranger to each other it would not have be proper to have let you into too many circumstance relate to myself all at once
you must have a little patience
i have undertake you see to write not only my life but my opinion also hop and expect that your knowledge of my character and of what kind of a mortal i be by the one would give you a well relish for the other as you proceed far with i the slight acquaintance which be now begin betwixt we will grow into familiarity and that unless one of we be in fault will terminate in friendship o diem praeclarum then nothing which have touch i will be think trifle in its nature or tedious in its telling
therefore my dear friend and companion if you should think i somewhat sparing of my narrative on my first set out--bear with me and let i go on and tell my story my own way or if i should seem now and then to trifle upon the road or should sometimes put on a fool s cap with a bell to it for a moment or two as we pass along don t fly off but rather courteously give i credit for a little more wisdom than appear upon my outside and as we jog on either laugh with i or at i or in short do any thing only keep your temper
chapter 1 vii
in the same village where my father and my mother dwelt dwelt also a thin upright motherly notable good old body of a midwife who with the help of a little plain good sense and some year full employment in her business in which she have all along trust little to her own effort and a great deal to those of dame nature had acquire in her way no small degree of reputation in the world by which word world need i in this place inform your worship that i would be understand to mean no more of it than a small circle describe upon the circle of the great world of four english mile diameter or thereabout of which the cottage where the good old woman live be suppose to be the centre she have be leave it seem a widow in great distress with three or four small child in her forty-seventh year and as she be at that time a person of decent carriage grave deportment a woman moreover of few word and withal an object of compassion whose distress and silence under it call out the loud for a friendly lift the wife of the parson of the parish be touch with pity and have often lament an inconvenience to which her husband s flock have for many year be expose inasmuch as there be no such thing as a midwife of any kind or degree to be get at let the case have be never so urgent within less than six or seven long mile rid which say seven long mile in dark night and dismal road the country thereabout be nothing but a deep clay be almost equal to fourteen and that in effect be sometimes next to have no midwife at all it come into her head that it would be do as seasonable a kindness to the whole parish as to the poor creature herself to get she a little instruct in some of the plain principle of the business in order to set she up in it
as no woman thereabout be well qualified to execute the plan she have form than herself the gentlewoman very charitably undertake it and have great influence over the female part of the parish she find no difficulty in effect it to the utmost of her wish
in truth the parson join d his interest with his wife s in the whole affair and in order to do thing as they should be and give the poor soul as good a title by law to practise as his wife have give by institution he cheerfully pay the fee for the ordinary s licence himself amount in the whole to the sum of eighteen shilling and four penny so that betwixt they both the good woman be fully invest in the real and corporal possession of her office together with all its right member and appurtenance whatsoever
these last word you must know be not accord to the old form in which such licence faculty and power usually run which in like case have heretofore be grant to the sisterhood
but it be accord to a neat formula of didius his own devising who have a particular turn for take to piece and new framing over again all kind of instrument in that way not only hit upon this dainty amendment but coax many of the old licensed matron in the neighbourhood to open their faculty afresh in order to have this wham-wham of his insert
i own i never could envy didius in these kind of fancy of his but every man to his own taste did not dr
kunastrokius that great man at his leisure hour take the great delight imaginable in comb of ass tail and pluck the dead hair out with his tooth though he have tweezer always in his pocket
nay if you come to that sir have not the wise of man in all age not except solomon himself have they not have their hobby-horses their run horses their coin and their cockle-shell their drum and their trumpet their fiddle their pallets their maggot and their butterflies and so long as a man rid his hobby-horse peaceably and quietly along the king s highway and neither compel you or i to get up behind him pray sir what have either you or i to do with it
chapter 1 viii
de gustibus non est disputandum that be there be no disputing against hobby-horse and for my part i seldom do nor could i with any sort of grace have i be an enemy to they at the bottom for happen at certain interval and change of the moon to be both fidler and painter accord as the fly stings be it know to you that i keep a couple of pad myself upon which in their turn nor do i care who know it i frequently ride out and take the air though sometimes to my shame be it speak i take somewhat long journie than what a wise man would think altogether right but the truth is i be not a wise man and besides be a mortal of so little consequence in the world it be not much matter what i do so i seldom fret or fume at all about it nor do it much disturb my rest when i see such great lords and tall personages as hereafter follow such for instance as my lord a b c d e f g h i k l m n o p q and so on all of a row mount upon their several horses some with large stirrup get on in a more grave and sober pace other on the contrary tuck up to their very chin with whip across their mouth scour and scamper it away like so many little party- colour devil astride a mortgage and as if some of they be resolve to break their necks so much the well--say i to myself for in case the bad should happen the world will make a shift to do excellently well without they and for the rest why--god speed they--e en let they ride on without opposition from i for be their lordship unhorse this very night-- tis ten to one but that many of they would be bad mount by one half before tomorrow morning
not one of these instance therefore can be say to break in upon my rest but there be an instance which i own put i off my guard and that be when i see one bear for great action and what be still more for his honour whose nature ever incline he to good ones when i behold such a one my lord like yourself whose principle and conduct be as generous and noble as his blood and whom for that reason a corrupt world cannot spare one moment when i see such a one my lord mount though it be but for a minute beyond the time which my love to my country have prescribe to he and my zeal for his glory wishes then my lord i cease to be a philosopher and in the first transport of an honest impatience i wish the hobby-horse with all his fraternity at the devil
my lord i maintain this to be a dedication notwithstanding its singularity in the three great essential of matter form and place i beg therefore you will accept it as such and that you will permit i to lay it with the most respectful humility at your lordship s foot--when you be upon them which you can be when you please and that be my lord whenever there be occasion for it and i will add to the good purpose too
i have the honour to be my lord your lordship s most obedient and most devoted and most humble servant tristram shandy chapter 1 ix
i solemnly declare to all mankind that the above dedication be make for no one prince prelate pope or potentate duke marquis earl viscount or baron of this or any other realm in christendom nor have it yet be hawk about or offer publicly or privately directly or indirectly to any one person or personage great or small but be honestly a true virgin- dedication untried on upon any soul live
i labour this point so particularly merely to remove any offence or objection which might arise against it from the manner in which i propose to make the most of it which be the put it up fairly to public sale which i now do
every author have a way of his own in bring his point to bear for my own part as i hate chaffer and higgle for a few guineas in a dark entry i resolve within myself from the very beginning to deal squarely and openly with your great folks in this affair and try whether i should not come off the well by it
if therefore there be any one duke marquis earl viscount or baron in these his majesty s dominion who stand in need of a tight genteel dedication and whom the above will suit for by the bye unless it suit in some degree i will not part with it it be much at his service for fifty guineas which i be positive be twenty guineas less than it ought to be afford for by any man of genius
my lord if you examine it over again it be far from be a gross piece of daubing as some dedication be
the design your lordship see be good the colour transparent the drawing not amiss or to speak more like a man of science and measure my piece in the painter s scale divide into 20 i believe my lord the outline will turn out as 12 the composition as 9 the colouring as 6 the expression 13 and a half and the design if i may be allow my lord to understand my own design and suppose absolute perfection in designing to be as 20 i think it cannot well fall short of 19
besides all this there be keep in it and the dark stroke in the hobby-horse which be a secondary figure and a kind of back-ground to the whole give great force to the principal light in your own figure and make it come off wonderfully and besides there be an air of originality in the tout ensemble
be pleased my good lord to order the sum to be pay into the hand of mr
dodsley for the benefit of the author and in the next edition care shall be take that this chapter be expunge and your lordship s title distinction arm and good action be place at the front of the precede chapter all which from the word de gustibus non est disputandum and whatever else in this book relate to hobby-horse but no more shall stand dedicated to your lordship the rest i dedicate to the moon who by the bye of all the patrons or matrons i can think of have most power to set my book a-go and make the world run mad after it
bright goddess if thou art not too busy with candid and miss cunegund s affairs take tristram shandy s under thy protection also
chapter 1 x
whatever degree of small merit the act of benignity in favour of the midwife might justly claim or in whom that claim truly rested at first sight seem not very material to this history certain however it be that the gentlewoman the parson s wife do run away at that time with the whole of it and yet for my life i cannot help think but that the parson himself though he have not the good fortune to hit upon the design first yet as he heartily concur in it the moment it be lay before he and as heartily part with his money to carry it into execution have a claim to some share of it if not to a full half of whatever honour be due to it
the world at that time be pleased to determine the matter otherwise
lay down the book and i will allow you half a day to give a probable guess at the ground of this procedure
be it know then that for about five year before the date of the midwife s licence of which you have have so circumstantial an account the parson we have to do with have make himself a country-talk by a breach of all decorum which he have commit against himself his station and his office and that be in never appear well or otherwise mount than upon a lean sorry jackass of a horse value about one pound fifteen shilling who to shorten all description of he be full brother to rosinante as far as similitude congenial could make he for he answer his description to a hair-breadth in every thing except that i do not remember tis any where say that rosinante be break-wind and that moreover rosinante as be the happiness of most spanish horse fat or lean wa undoubtedly a horse at all point
i know very well that the hero s horse be a horse of chaste deportment which may have give ground for the contrary opinion but it be as certain at the same time that rosinante s continency as may be demonstrate from the adventure of the yanguesian carrier proceed from no bodily defect or cause whatsoever but from the temperance and orderly current of his blood and let i tell you madam there be a great deal of very good chastity in the world in behalf of which you could not say more for your life
let that be as it may as my purpose be to do exact justice to every creature bring upon the stage of this dramatic work i could not stifle this distinction in favour of don quixote s horse in all other point the parson s horse i say be just such another for he be as lean and as lank and as sorry a jade as humility herself could have bestride
in the estimation of here and there a man of weak judgment it be greatly in the parson s power to have help the figure of this horse of his for he be master of a very handsome demi-peak saddle quilt on the seat with green plush garnish with a double row of silver-head stud and a noble pair of shin brass stirrup with a housing altogether suitable of grey superfine cloth with an edging of black lace terminate in a deep black silk fringe poudre d or all which he have purchase in the pride and prime of his life together with a grand embossed bridle ornament at all point as it should be but not care to banter his beast he have hang all these up behind his study door and in lieu of they have seriously befit he with just such a bridle and such a saddle as the figure and value of such a steed might well and truly deserve
in the several sally about his parish and in the neighbouring visit to the gentry who live around him you will easily comprehend that the parson so appoint would both hear and see enough to keep his philosophy from rusting
to speak the truth he never could enter a village but he catch the attention of both old and young labour stand still as he pass d--the bucket hung suspend in the middle of the well the spinning- wheel forget its round even chuck-farthe and shuffle-cap themselves stand gap till he have get out of sight and as his movement be not of the quick he have generally time enough upon his hand to make his observations to hear the groan of the serious and the laughter of the light-hearted all which he bear with excellent tranquillity hi character was he love a jest in his heart--and as he see himself in the true point of ridicule he would say he could not be angry with other for see he in a light in which he so strongly see himself so that to his friend who know his foible be not the love of money and who therefore make the less scruple in banter the extravagance of his humour instead of give the true cause he choose rather to join in the laugh against himself and as he never carry one single ounce of flesh upon his own bone be altogether as spare a figure as his beast he would sometimes insist upon it that the horse be as good as the rider deserved that they be centaur-like both of a piece
at other time and in other mood when his spirit be above the temptation of false wit he would say he find himself go off fast in a consumption and with great gravity would pretend he could not bear the sight of a fat horse without a dejection of heart and a sensible alteration in his pulse and that he have make choice of the lean one he ride upon not only to keep himself in countenance but in spirit
at different time he would give fifty humorous and apposite reason for rid a meek-spirited jade of a break-wind horse preferably to one of mettle for on such a one he could sit mechanically and meditate as delightfully de vanitate mundi et fuga faeculi as with the advantage of a death s-head before him that in all other exercitation he could spend his time as he ride slowly along to as much account as in his study that he could draw up an argument in his sermon or a hole in his breech as steadily on the one as in the other that brisk trotting and slow argumentation like wit and judgment be two incompatible movements but that upon his steed--he could unite and reconcile every thing he could compose his sermon--he could compose his cough and in case nature give a call that way he could likewise compose himself to sleep in short the parson upon such encounter would assign any cause but the true cause and he with-hold the true one only out of a nicety of temper because he think it do honour to he
but the truth of the story be as follow in the first year of this gentleman s life and about the time when the superb saddle and bridle be purchase by he it have be his manner or vanity or call it what you will to run into the opposite extreme in the language of the county where he dwelt he be say to have love a good horse and generally have one of the good in the whole parish stand in his stable always ready for saddling and as the near midwife as i tell you do not live near to the village than seven mile and in a vile country it so fall out that the poor gentleman be scarce a whole week together without some piteous application for his beast and as he be not an unkind-hearted man and every case be more pressing and more distressful than the last as much as he love his beast he have never a heart to refuse he the upshot of which be generally this that his horse be either clapp d or spavin d or greaz d or he be twitter-bon d or break-wind or something in short or other have befall he which would let he carry no flesh so that he have every nine or ten month a bad horse to get rid of and a good horse to purchase in his stead
what the loss in such a balance might amount to communibus annis i would leave to a special jury of sufferer in the same traffick to determine but let it be what it would the honest gentleman bear it for many year without a murmur till at length by repeat ill accident of the kind he find it necessary to take the thing under consideration and upon weigh the whole and sum it up in his mind he find it not only disproportione to his other expence but withal so heavy an article in itself as to disable he from any other act of generosity in his parish besides this he consider that with half the sum thus gallop away he could do ten time as much good and what still weigh more with he than all other consideration put together be this that it confine all his charity into one particular channel and where as he fancy it be the least want namely to the child-bearing and child-get part of his parish reserve nothing for the impotent nothing for the aged nothing for the many comfortless scene he be hourly call forth to visit where poverty and sickness and affliction dwelt together
for these reason he resolve to discontinue the expence and there appear but two possible way to extricate he clearly out of it and these be either to make it an irrevocable law never more to lend his steed upon any application whatever or else be content to ride the last poor devil such as they have make he with all his ache and infirmity to the very end of the chapter
as he dread his own constancy in the first--he very chearfully betook himself to the second and though he could very well have explain it as i say to his honour yet for that very reason he have a spirit above it choose rather to bear the contempt of his enemy and the laughter of his friend than undergo the pain of tell a story which might seem a panegyrick upon himself
i have the high idea of the spiritual and refined sentiment of this reverend gentleman from this single stroke in his character which i think come up to any of the honest refinement of the peerless knight of la mancha whom by the bye with all his folly i love more and would actually have go far to have pay a visit to than the great hero of antiquity
but this be not the moral of my story the thing i have in view be to shew the temper of the world in the whole of this affair for you must know that so long as this explanation would have do the parson credit the devil a soul could find it out i suppose his enemy would not and that his friend could not but no soon do he bestir himself in behalf of the midwife and pay the expence of the ordinary s licence to set her up but the whole secret come out every horse he have lose and two horse more than ever he have lose with all the circumstance of their destruction be know and distinctly remembered the story run like wild-fire the parson have a return fit of pride which have just seize he and he be go to be well mount once again in his life and if it be so twa plain as the sun at noon-day he would pocket the expence of the licence ten time tell the very first year so that every body be leave to judge what be his view in this act of charity what be his view in this and in every other action of his life or rather what be the opinion which float in the brain of other people concern it be a thought which too much float in his own and too often break in upon his rest when he should have be sound asleep
about ten year ago this gentleman have the good fortune to be make entirely easy upon that score it be just so long since he leave his parish and the whole world at the same time behind him and stand accountable to a judge of whom he will have no cause to complain
but there be a fatality attend the action of some man order they as they will they pass thro a certain medium which so twist and refract they from their true direction--that with all the title to praise which a rectitude of heart can give the doer of they be nevertheless force to live and die without it
of the truth of which this gentleman be a painful example but to know by what mean this come to pass and to make that knowledge of use to you i insist upon it that you read the two follow chapter which contain such a sketch of his life and conversation as will carry its moral along with it when this be do if nothing stop we in our way we will go on with the midwife
chapter 1 xi
yorick be this parson s name and what be very remarkable in it as appear from a most ancient account of the family write upon strong vellum and now in perfect preservation it have be exactly so spelt for near i be within an ace of say nine hundred years but i would not shake my credit in tell an improbable truth however indisputable in itself and therefore i shall content myself with only say--it have be exactly so spelt without the least variation or transposition of a single letter for i do not know how long which be more than i would venture to say of one half of the good surname in the kingdom which in a course of year have generally undergo as many chop and change as their owners has this be owe to the pride or to the shame of the respective proprietors in honest truth i think sometimes to the one and sometimes to the other just as the temptation have work
but a villainous affair it be and will one day so blend and confound we all together that no one shall be able to stand up and swear that his own great grandfather be the man who do either this or that this evil have be sufficiently fence against by the prudent care of the yorick s family and their religious preservation of these record i quote which do far inform we that the family be originally of danish extraction and have be transplant into england as early as in the reign of horwendillus king of denmark in whose court it seem an ancestor of this mr
yorick s and from whom he be lineally descend hold a considerable post to the day of his death
of what nature this considerable post be this record saith not it only add that for near two century it have be totally abolish as altogether unnecessary not only in that court but in every other court of the christian world
it have often come into my head that this post could be no other than that of the king s chief jester and that hamlet s yorick in our shakespeare many of whose play you know be found upon authenticated fact be certainly the very man
i have not the time to look into saxo-grammaticus s danish history to know the certainty of this but if you have leisure and can easily get at the book you may do it full as well yourself
i have just time in my travel through denmark with mr
noddy s eld son whom in the year 1741 i accompany as governor rid along with he at a prodigious rate thro most part of europe and of which original journey perform by us two a most delectable narrative will be give in the progress of this work
i have just time i say and that be all to prove the truth of an observation make by a long sojourner in that country namely that nature be neither very lavish nor be she very stingy in her gift of genius and capacity to its inhabitants but like a discreet parent be moderately kind to they all observe such an equal tenor in the distribution of her favour as to bring they in those point pretty near to a level with each other so that you will meet with few instance in that kingdom of refined part but a great deal of good plain houshold understanding amongst all rank of people of which every body have a share which be i think very right
with we you see the case be quite different we be all up and down in this matter you be a great genius or tis fifty to one sir you be a great dunce and a blockhead not that there be a total want of intermediate steps no we be not so irregular as that come to but the two extreme be more common and in a great degree in this unsettled island where nature in her gift and disposition of this kind be most whimsical and capricious fortune herself not be more so in the bequest of her good and chattel than she
this be all that ever stagger my faith in regard to yorick s extraction who by what i can remember of he and by all the account i could ever get of he seem not to have have one single drop of danish blood in his whole crasis in nine hundred year it might possibly have all run out i will not philosophize one moment with you about it for happen how it would the fact be this that instead of that cold phlegm and exact regularity of sense and humour you would have look for in one so extracted he be on the contrary as mercurial and sublimate a composition as heteroclite a creature in all his declensions with as much life and whim and gaite de coeur about he as the kindliest climate could have engender and put together
with all this sail poor yorick carry not one ounce of ballast he be utterly unpractised in the world and at the age of twenty-six know just about as well how to steer his course in it as a romping unsuspicious girl of thirteen so that upon his first set out the brisk gale of his spirit as you will imagine run he foul ten time in a day of somebody s tackle and as the grave and more slow-pace be often in his way you may likewise imagine twa with such he have generally the ill luck to get the most entangle
for aught i know there might be some mixture of unlucky wit at the bottom of such fracas for to speak the truth yorick have an invincible dislike and opposition in his nature to gravity not to gravity as such for where gravity be want he would be the most grave or serious of mortal man for day and week together but he be an enemy to the affectation of it and declare open war against it only as it appear a cloak for ignorance or for folly and then whenever it fall in his way however shelter and protect he seldom give it much quarter
sometimes in his wild way of talk he would say that gravity be an errant scoundrel and he would add of the most dangerous kind too because a sly one and that he verily believe more honest well-mean people be bubble out of their good and money by it in one twelve-month than by pocket-picking and shop-lifting in seven
in the naked temper which a merry heart discover he would say there be no danger but to itself whereas the very essence of gravity be design and consequently deceit twa a taught trick to gain credit of the world for more sense and knowledge than a man be worth and that with all its pretensions it be no well but often bad than what a french wit have long ago define it viz
a mysterious carriage of the body to cover the defect of the mind which definition of gravity yorick with great imprudence would say deserve to be write in letter of gold
but in plain truth he be a man unhackneyed and unpractise in the world and be altogether as indiscreet and foolish on every other subject of discourse where policy be willnot to impress restraint
yorick have no impression but one and that be what arise from the nature of the deed speak of which impression he would usually translate into plain english without any periphrasis and too oft without much distinction of either person time or place so that when mention be make of a pitiful or an ungenerous proceeding--he never give himself a moment s time to reflect who be the hero of the piece what his station or how far he have power to hurt he hereafter but if it be a dirty action without more ado the man be a dirty fellow and so on and as his comment have usually the ill fate to be terminate either in a bon mot or to be enliven throughout with some drollery or humour of expression it give wing to yorick s indiscretion
in a word tho he never seek yet at the same time as he seldom shun occasion of say what come uppermost and without much ceremony he have but too many temptation in life of scatter his wit and his humour his gibe and his jest about him they be not lose for want of gathering
what be the consequence and what be yorick s catastrophe thereupon you will read in the next chapter
chapter 1 xii
the mortgager and mortgagee differ the one from the other not more in length of purse than the jester and jestee do in that of memory
but in this the comparison between they run as the scholiast call it upon all- four which by the bye be upon one or two leg more than some of the good of homer s can pretend to namely that the one raise a sum and the other a laugh at your expence and think no more about it
interest however still run on in both cases the periodical or accidental payment of it just serve to keep the memory of the affair alive till at length in some evil hour pop come the creditor upon each and by demand principal upon the spot together with full interest to the very day make they both feel the full extent of their obligation
as the reader for i hate your if have a thorough knowledge of human nature i need not say more to satisfy he that my hero could not go on at this rate without some slight experience of these incidental memento

eugenius would never admit this and would often tell he that one day or other he would certainly be reckon with and he would often add in an accent of sorrowful apprehension to the uttermost mite

trust i dear yorick this unwary pleasantry of thine will soon or later bring thee into scrape and difficulty which no after-wit can extricate thee out of in these sally too oft i see it happen that a person laugh at consider himself in the light of a person injure with all the right of such a situation belong to he and when thou viewest he in that light too and reckon up his friend his family his kindre and allies and muster up with they the many recruit which will list under he from a sense of common danger ti no extravagant arithmetic to say that for every ten jokes thou hast get an hundred enemy and till thou hast go on and raise a swarm of wasp about thine ear and art half sting to death by they thou wilt never be convince it be so
i cannot suspect it in the man whom i esteem that there be the least spur from spleen or malevolence of intent in these sally--i believe and know they to be truly honest and sportive but consider my dear lad that fool cannot distinguish this and that knave will not and thou know not what it be either to provoke the one or to make merry with the other whenever they associate for mutual defence depend upon it they will carry on the war in such a manner against thee my dear friend as to make thee heartily sick of it and of thy life too
revenge from some baneful corner shall level a tale of dishonour at thee which no innocence of heart or integrity of conduct shall set right the fortune of thy house shall totter thy character which lead the way to they shall bleed on every side of it thy faith questioned thy work belied thy wit forgotten thy learning trample on
to wind up the last scene of thy tragedy cruelty and cowardice twin ruffian hire and set on by malice in the dark shall strike together at all thy infirmity and mistakes the good of we my dear lad lie open there and trust me trust i yorick when to gratify a private appetite it be once resolve upon that an innocent and an helpless creature shall be sacrifice tis an easy matter to pick up stick enough from any thicket where it have stray to make a fire to offer it up with

full surely preferment be o ripening they have smote his root and then he fall as many a worthy man have fall before he

chapter 1 xiii
it be so long since the reader of this rhapsodical work have be part from the midwife that it be high time to mention she again to he merely to put he in mind that there be such a body still in the world and whom upon the good judgment i can form upon my own plan at present i be go to introduce to he for good and all but as fresh matter may be start and much unexpected business fall out betwixt the reader and myself which may require immediate dispatch twa right to take care that the poor woman should not be lose in the mean time because when she be want we can no way do without she
i think i tell you that this good woman be a person of no small note and consequence throughout our whole village and township that her fame have spread itself to the very out-edge and circumference of that circle of importance of which kind every soul live whether he have a shirt to his back or no ha one surround him which say circle by the way whenever tis say that such a one be of great weight and importance in the world i desire may be enlarge or contract in your worship s fancy in a compound ratio of the station profession knowledge ability height and depth measure both way of the personage bring before you
in the present case if i remember i fix it about four or five mile which not only comprehend the whole parish but extend itself to two or three of the adjacent hamlet in the skirt of the next parish which make a considerable thing of it
i must add that she be moreover very well look on at one large grange-house and some other odd house and farm within two or three mile as i say from the smoke of her own chimney but i must here once for all inform you that all this will be more exactly delineated and explain d in a map now in the hand of the engraver which with many other piece and developement of this work will be add to the end of the twentieth volume not to swell the work i det the thought of such a thing but by way of commentary scholium illustration and key to such passage incident or inuendo as shall be think to be either of private interpretation or of dark or doubtful meaning after my life and my opinion shall have be read over now donot forget the meaning of the word by all the world which betwixt you and i and in spite of all the gentleman-reviewer in great britain and of all that their worship shall undertake to write or say to the contrary i be determined shall be the case i need not tell your worship that all this be speak in confidence
chapter 1 xiv
upon look into my mother s marriage settlement in order to satisfy myself and reader in a point necessary to be clear up before we could proceed any far in this history i have the good fortune to pop upon the very thing i want before i have read a day and a half straight forwards it might have take i up a month which shews plainly that when a man sit down to write a history tho it be but the history of jack hickathrift or tom thumb he know no more than his heel what let and confound hindrance he be to meet with in his way or what a dance he may be lead by one excursion or another before all be over
could a historiographer drive on his history as a muleteer drive on his mule straight forward for instance from rome all the way to loretto without ever once turn his head aside either to the right hand or to the left he might venture to foretell you to an hour when he should get to his journey s end but the thing be morally speak impossible for if he be a man of the least spirit he will have fifty deviation from a straight line to make with this or that party as he go along which he can no way avoid
he will have view and prospect to himself perpetually solicit his eye which he can no more help stand still to look at than he can fly he will moreover have various account to reconcile anecdote to pick up inscription to make out story to weave in tradition to sift personage to call upon panegyrick to paste up at this door pasquinades at that all which both the man and his mule be quite exempt from
to sum up all there be archive at every stage to be look d into and roll record document and endless genealogy which justice ever and anon call he back to stay the reading of in short there be no end of it for my own part i declare i have be at it these six week make all the speed i possibly could and be not yet born i have just be able and thatbe all to tell you when it happen d but not how so that you see the thing be yet far from be accomplish
these unforeseen stoppage which i own i have no conception of when i first set out but which i be convince now will rather increase than diminish as i advance have strike out a hint which i be resolve to follow and that is not to be in a hurry but to go on leisurely writing and publish two volume of my life every year which if i be suffer to go on quietly and can make a tolerable bargain with my bookseller i shall continue to do as long as i live

chapter 1 xvi
my father as any body may naturally imagine come down with my mother into the country in but a pettish kind of a humour
the first twenty or five- and-twenty mile he do nothing in the world but fret and teaze himself and indeed my mother too about the cursed expence which he say might every shilling of it have be saved then what vex he more than every thing else be the provoke time of the year which as i tell you be towards the end of september when his wall-fruit and green gage especially in which he be very curious be just ready for pulling have he be whistle up to london upon a tom fool s errand in any other month of the whole year he should not have say three word about it for the next two whole stage no subject would go down but the heavy blow he have sustain d from the loss of a son whom it seem he have fully reckon d upon in his mind and register d down in his pocket-book as a second staff for his old age in case bobby should fail he

chapter 1 xviii

he be very sensible that all political writer upon the subject have unanimously agree and lament from the beginning of queen elizabeth s reign down to his own time that the current of man and money towards the metropolis upon one frivolous errand or another set in so strong as to become dangerous to our civil rights though by the bye a current be not the image he take most delight in a distemper be here his favourite metaphor and he would run it down into a perfect allegory by maintain it be identically the same in the body national as in the body natural where the blood and spirit be drive up into the head fast than they could find their way down a stoppage of circulation must ensue which be death in both case
there be little danger he would say of lose our liberty by french politick or french invasions nor be he so much in pain of a consumption from the mass of corrupted matter and ulcerated humour in our constitution which he hop be not so bad as it be imagined but he verily fear that in some violent push we should go off all at once in a state-apoplexy and then he would say the lord have mercy upon we all
my father be never able to give the history of this distemper without the remedy along with it
be i an absolute prince he would say pull up his breech with both his hand as he rise from his arm-chair i would appoint able judge at every avenue of my metropolis who should take cognizance of every fool s business who come there and if upon a fair and candid hearing it appear not of weight sufficient to leave his own home and come up bag and baggage with his wife and child farmer s son c

at his backside they should be all send back from constable to constable like vagrant as they be to the place of their legal settlement

this contrariety of humour betwixt my father and my uncle be the source of many a fraternal squabble

for god s sake my uncle toby would cry and for my sake and for all our sake my dear brother shandy do let this story of our aunt s and her ash sleep in peace how can you how can you have so little feeling and compassion for the character of our family what be the character of a family to an hypothesis
my father would reply nay if you come to that-- what be the life of a family the life of a family my uncle toby would say throw himself back in his arm chair and lift up his hand his eye and one leg--yes the life my father would say maintain his point
how many thousand of them be there every year that come cast away in all civilized country at least and consider as nothing but common air in competition of an hypothesis
in my plain sense of thing my uncle toby would answer every such instance be downright murder let who will commit it there lie your mistake my father would reply for in foro scientiae there be no such thing as murder tis only death brother
my uncle toby would never offer to answer this by any other kind of argument than that of whistle half a dozen bar of lillebullero you must know it be the usual channel thro which his passion get vent when any thing shock or surprized him but especially when any thing which he deem d very absurd be offer
as not one of our logical writer nor any of the commentator upon they that i remember have think proper to give a name to this particular specie of argument i here take the liberty to do it myself for two reason
first that in order to prevent all confusion in dispute it may stand as much distinguished for ever from every other specie of argument--as the argumentum ad verecundiam ex absurdo ex fortiori or any other argument whatsoever and secondly that it may be say by my child s child when my head be lay to rest that their learn d grandfather s head have be busy to as much purpose once as other people s that he have invent a name and generously throw it into the treasury of the ars logica for one of the most unanswerable argument in the whole science
and if the end of disputation be more to silence than convince they may add if they please to one of the good argument too
i do therefore by these present strictly order and command that it be know and distinguish by the name and title of the argumentum fistulatorium and no other and that it rank hereafter with the argumentum baculinum and the argumentum ad crumenam and for ever hereafter be treat of in the same chapter
as for the argumentum tripodium which be never use but by the woman against the man and the argumentum ad rem which contrarywise be make use of by the man only against the woman as these two be enough in conscience for one lecture and moreover as the one be the good answer to the other let they likewise be keep apart and be treat of in a place by themselves

this be vile work for which reason from the beginning of this you see i have construct the main work and the adventitious part of it with such intersection and have so complicated and involve the digressive and progressive movement one wheel within another that the whole machine in general have be keep a-going and whatbe more it shall be keep a-go these forty year if it please the fountain of health to bless i so long with life and good spirit

matter of no more seeming consequence in themselves than whether my father should have take off his wig with his right hand or with his left have divide the great kingdom and make the crown of the monarch who govern they to totter upon their heads but need i tell you sir that the circumstance with which every thing in this world be begirt give every thing in this world its size and shape and by tighten it or relax it this way or that make the thing to be what it be--great--little--good--bad--indifferent or not indifferent just as the case happen

i have but two thing to observe of paraeus first that he prove and explain all this with the utmost chastity and decorum of expression for which may his soul for ever rest in peace

slawkenbergius s tale it be one cool refresh evening at the close of a very sultry day in the latter end of the month of august when a stranger mount upon a dark mule with a small cloak-bag behind he contain a few shirt a pair of shoe and a crimson-sattin pair of breech enter the town of strasburg
he tell the centinel who question he as he enter the gate that he have be at the promontory of noses--be go on to frankfort--and should be back again at strasburg that day month in his way to the border of crim tartary
the centinel look up into the stranger s face--he never see such a nose in his life
i have make a very good venture of it quoth the stranger--so slip his wrist out of the loop of a black ribbon to which a short scymetar be hung he put his hand into his pocket and with great courtesy touch the fore part of his cap with his left hand as he extend his right--he put a florin into the centinel s hand and pass on
it grieve i say the centinel speak to a little dwarfish bandy- legg d drummer that so courteous a soul should have lose his scabbard--he cannot travel without one to his scymetar and will not be able to get a scabbard to fit it in all strasburg i never have one reply the stranger look back to the centinel and put his hand up to his cap as he speak--i carry it continue he thus--hold up his naked scymetar his mule move on slowly all the time--on purpose to defend my nose
it be well worth it gentle stranger reply the centinel
ti not worth a single stiver say the bandy-legg d drummer-- tis a nose of parchment
as i be a true catholic--except that it be six time as big-- tis a nose say the centinel like my own
i hear it crackle say the drummer
by dunder say the centinel i see it bleed
what a pity cry the bandy-legg d drummer we do not both touch it
at the very time that this dispute be maintain by the centinel and the drummer--be the same point debate betwixt a trumpeter and a trumpeter s wife who be just then come up and have stop to see the stranger pass by
benedicity what a nose
tis as long say the trumpeter s wife as a trumpet
and of the same metal say the trumpeter as you hear by its sneezing
tis as soft as a flute say she
tis brass say the trumpeter

the neglect of this punctilio in phutatorius which by-the-bye should be a warning to all mankind have open a door to this accident accident i call it in compliance to a receive mode of speak--but in no opposition to the opinion either of acrites or mythogeras in this matter i know they be both prepossess and fully persuade of it--and be so to this hour that there be nothing of accident in the whole event--but that the chesnut s take that particular course and in a manner of its own accord--and then fall with all its heat directly into that one particular place and no other--be a real judgment upon phutatorius for that filthy and obscene treatise de concubinis retinendi which phutatorius have publish about twenty year ago--and be that identical week go to give the world a second edition of
it be not my business to dip my pen in this controversy--much undoubtedly may be write on both side of the question--all that concern i as an historian be to represent the matter of fact and render it credible to the reader that the hiatus in phutatorius s breech be sufficiently wide to receive the chesnut and that the chesnut somehow or other do fall perpendicularly and pip hot into it without phutatorius s perceiving it or any one else at that time
the genial warmth which the chesnut impart be not undelectable for the first twenty or five-and-twenty second--and do no more than gently solicit phutatorius s attention towards the part but the heat gradually increase and in a few second more get beyond the point of all sober pleasure and then advance with all speed into the region of pain the soul of phutatorius together with all his idea his thought his attention his imagination judgment resolution deliberation ratiocination memory fancy with ten battalion of animal spirit all tumultuously crowd down through different defile and circuit to the place of danger leave all his upper region as you may imagine as empty as my purse
with the good intelligence which all these messenger could bring he back phutatorius be not able to dive into the secret of what be go forwards below nor could he make any kind of conjecture what the devil be the matter with it however as he know not what the true cause might turn out he deem it most prudent in the situation he be in at present to bear it if possible like a stoick which with the help of some wry face and compursion of the mouth he have certainly accomplish have his imagination continue neuter but the sally of the imagination be ungovernable in thing of this kind--a thought instantly dart into his mind that tho the anguish have the sensation of glow heat--it might notwithstanding that be a bite as well as a burn and if so that possibly a newt or an asker or some such detest reptile have creep up and be fasten his tooth--the horrid idea of which with a fresh glow of pain arise that instant from the chesnut seize phutatorius with a sudden panick and in the first terrifying disorder of the passion it throw he as it have do the good general upon earth quite off his guard the effect of which be this that he leap incontinently up utter as he rise that interjection of surprise so much descanted upon with the aposiopestic break after it mark thus z ds--which though not strictly canonical be still as little as any man could have say upon the occasion and which by-the-bye whether canonical or not phutatorius could no more help than he could the cause of it
though this have take up some time in the narrative it take up little more time in the transaction than just to allow time for phutatorius to draw forth the chesnut and throw it down with violence upon the floor--and for yorick to rise from his chair and pick the chesnut up
it be curious to observe the triumph of slight incident over the mind what incredible weight they have in form and govern our opinion both of man and thing--that trifle light as air shall waft a belief into the soul and plant it so immoveably within it--that euclid s demonstration could they be bring to batter it in breach should not all have power to overthrow it
yorick i say pick up the chesnut which phutatorius s wrath have fling down--the action be trifle--i be ashamed to account for it--he do it for no reason but that he think the chesnut not a jot bad for the adventure--and that he hold a good chesnut worth stoop for but this incident trifle as it be work differently in phutatorius s head he consider this act of yorick s in get off his chair and pick up the chesnut as a plain acknowledgment in he that the chesnut be originally his--and in course that it must have be the owner of the chesnut and no one else who could have play he such a prank with it what greatly confirm he in this opinion be this that the table be parallelogramical and very narrow it afford a fair opportunity for yorick who sit directly over against phutatorius of slip the chesnut in--and consequently that he do it
the look of something more than suspicion which phutatorius cast full upon yorick as these thought arise too evidently speak his opinion--and as phutatorius be naturally suppose to know more of the matter than any person besides his opinion at once become the general one and for a reason very different from any which have be yet give--in a little time it be put out of all manner of dispute
when great or unexpected event fall out upon the stage of this sublunary world--the mind of man which be an inquisitive kind of a substance naturally take a flight behind the scene to see what be the cause and first spring of them the search be not long in this instance
it be well know that yorick have never a good opinion of the treatise which phutatorius have write de concubinis retinendi as a thing which he fear have do hurt in the world--and twa easily find out that there be a mystical meaning in yorick s prank--and that his chucking the chesnut hot into phutatorius s
be a sarcastical fling at his book-- the doctrine of which they say have enflame many an honest man in the same place
this conceit awaken d somnolentus--make agelastes smile--and if you can recollect the precise look and air of a man s face intent in find out a riddle--it throw gastripheres s into that form--and in short be think by many to be a master-stroke of arch-wit
this as the reader have see from one end to the other be as groundless as the dream of philosophy yorick no doubt as shakespeare say of his ancestor-- wa a man of jest but it be temper d with something which withhold he from that and many other ungracious prank of which he as undeservedly bear the blame but it be his misfortune all his life long to bear the imputation of say and do a thousand thing of which unless my esteem blind i his nature be incapable
all i blame he for--or rather all i blame and alternately like he for be that singularity of his temper which would never suffer he to take pain to set a story right with the world however in his power
in every ill usage of that sort he act precisely as in the affair of his lean horse--he could have explain it to his honour but his spirit be above it and besides he ever look upon the inventor the propagator and believer of an illiberal report alike so injurious to he--he could not stoop to tell his story to they--and so trust to time and truth to do it for he
this heroic cast produce he inconvenience in many respect--in the present it be follow by the fix resentment of phutatorius who as yorick have just make an end of his chesnut rise up from his chair a second time to let he know it--which indeed he do with a smile say only-- that he would endeavour not to forget the obligation
but you must mark and carefully separate and distinguish these two thing in your mind
the smile be for the company
the threat be for yorick

chapter 2 lxvi
though my father be hugely tickle with the subtlety of these learn discourses-- twa still but like the anointing of a broken bone--the moment he get home the weight of his affliction return upon he but so much the heavy as be ever the case when the staff we lean on slip from under us he become pensive--walk frequently forth to the fish-pond--let down one loop of his hat--sigh d often--forbore to snap--and as the hasty spark of temper which occasion snap so much assist perspiration and digestion as hippocrates tell we--he have certainly fall ill with the extinction of they have not his thought be critically draw off and his health rescue by a fresh train of disquietude leave he with a legacy of a thousand pound by my aunt dinah
my father have scarce read the letter when take the thing by the right end he instantly begin to plague and puzzle his head how to lay it out mostly to the honour of his family a hundred-and-fifty odd project take possession of his brain by turn--he would do this and that and t other-- he would go to rome--he would go to law--he would buy stock--he would buy john hobson s farm--he would new fore front his house and add a new wing to make it even--there be a fine water-mill on this side and he would build a wind-mill on the other side of the river in full view to answer it- but above all thing in the world he would inclose the great ox-moor and send out my brother bobby immediately upon his travel
but as the sum be finite and consequently could not do every thing--and in truth very few of these to any purpose--of all the project which offer themselves upon this occasion the two last seem to make the deep impression and he would infallibly have determine upon both at once but for the small inconvenience hint at above which absolutely put he under a necessity of decide in favour either of the one or the other
this be not altogether so easy to be do for though tis certain my father have long before set his heart upon this necessary part of my brother s education and like a prudent man have actually determine to carry it into execution with the first money that return from the second creation of action in the missisippi-scheme in which he be an adventurer--yet the ox-moor which be a fine large whinny undraine unimproved common belong to the shandy-estate have almost as old a claim upon he he have long and affectionately set his heart upon turn it likewise to some account
but have never hitherto be press with such a conjuncture of thing as make it necessary to settle either the priority or justice of their claim--like a wise man he have refrain enter into any nice or critical examination about they so that upon the dismission of every other project at this crisis--the two old project the ox-moor and my brother divide he again and so equal a match be they for each other as to become the occasion of no small contest in the old gentleman s mind--which of the two should be set o going first
people may laugh as they will--but the case be this
it have ever be the custom of the family and by length of time be almost become a matter of common right that the eld son of it should have free ingress egress and regress into foreign part before marriage--not only for the sake of better his own private part by the benefit of exercise and change of so much air--but simply for the mere delectation of his fancy by the feather put into his cap of have be abroad--tantum valet my father would say quantum sonat
now as this be a reasonable and in course a most christian indulgence--to deprive he of it without why or wherefore--and thereby make an example of he as the first shandy unwhirl d about europe in a post-chaise and only because he be a heavy lad--would be use he ten time bad than a turk
on the other hand the case of the ox-moor be full as hard
exclusive of the original purchase-money which be eight hundred pounds-- it have cost the family eight hundred pound more in a law-suit about fifteen year before--besides the lord know what trouble and vexation
it have be moreover in possession of the shandy-family ever since the middle of the last century and though it lie full in view before the house bound on one extremity by the water-mill and on the other by the project wind-mill speak of above--and for all these reason seem to have the fair title of any part of the estate to the care and protection of the family--yet by an unaccountable fatality common to man as well as the ground they tread on--it have all along most shamefully be overlook d and to speak the truth of it have suffer so much by it that it would have make any man s heart have bleed obadiah say who understand the value of the land to have ride over it and only see the condition it be in
however as neither the purchasing this tract of ground--nor indeed the placing of it where it lay be either of they properly speak of my father s do--he have never think himself any way concerned in the affair--till the fifteen year before when the breaking out of that curse law-suit mention above and which have arise about its boundaries which be altogether my father s own act and deed it naturally awaken every other argument in its favour and upon sum they all up together he see not merely in interest but in honour he be bind to do something for it--and that now or never be the time
i think there must certainly have be a mixture of ill-luck in it that the reason on both side should happen to be so equally balanced by each other for though my father weigh d they in all humour and conditions-- spend many an anxious hour in the most profound and abstract meditation upon what be good to be do--read book of farming one day--book of travel another--lay aside all passion whatever--view the argument on both side in all their light and circumstance--commune every day with my uncle toby--argue with yorick and talk over the whole affair of the ox-moor with obadiah--yet nothing in all that time appear so strongly in behalf of the one which be not either strictly applicable to the other or at least so far counterbalance by some consideration of equal weight as to keep the scale even
for to be sure with proper help in the hand of some people tho the ox-moor would undoubtedly have make a different appearance in the world from what it do or ever could do in the condition it lay--yet every tittle of this be true with regard to my brother bobby--let obadiah say what he would in point of interest--the contest i own at first sight do not appear so undecisive betwixt they for whenever my father take pen and ink in hand and set about calculate the simple expence of paring and burning and fencing in the ox-moor c

what be the life of man

chapter 3 lx
the ancient goth of germany who the learn cluverius be positive be first seat in the country between the vistula and the oder and who afterwards incorporate the herculi the bugians and some other vandallick clan to them--have all of they a wise custom of debate every thing of importance to their state twice that is once drunk and once sober drunk--that their council might not want vigour and sober--that they might not want discretion
now my father be entirely a water-drinker wa a long time gravel almost to death in turn this as much to his advantage as he do every other thing which the ancient do or say and it be not till the seventh year of his marriage after a thousand fruitless experiment and device that he hit upon an expedient which answer the purpose and that be when any difficult and momentous point be to be settle in the family which require great sobriety and great spirit too in its determination he fix and set apart the first sunday night in the month and the saturday night which immediately precede it to argue it over in bed with my mother by which contrivance if you consider sir with yourself
these my father humorously enough call his bed of justice for from the two different counsel take in these two different humour a middle one be generally find out which touch the point of wisdom as well as if he have get drunk and sober a hundred time
i must not be make a secret of to the world that this answer full as well in literary discussion as either in military or conjugal but it be not every author that can try the experiment as the goths and vandals do it-- or if he can may it be always for his body s health and to do it as my father do it am i sure it would be always for his soul s
my way be this in all nice and ticklish discussions of which heaven know there be but too many in my book where i find i cannot take a step without the danger of have either their worship or their reverence upon my back--i write one-half full and t other fasting or write it all full and correct it fasting or write it fasting and correct it full for they all come to the same thing so that with a less variation from my father s plan than my father s from the gothick--i feel myself upon a par with he in his first bed of justice and no way inferior to he in his second these different and almost irreconcileable effect flow uniformly from the wise and wonderful mechanism of nature of which be shebe the honour all that we can do be to turn and work the machine to the improvement and well manufactory of the art and sciences now when i write full i write as if i be never to write fast again as long as i live that be i write free from the care as well as the terror of the world i count not the number of my scars nor do my fancy go forth into dark entry and bye-corner to ante-date my stabs in a word my pen take its course and i write on as much from the fulness of my heart as my stomach but when an please your honour i indite fast tis a different history i pay the world all possible attention and respect and have as great a share whilst it last of that under strap virtue of discretion as the good of you so that betwixt both i write a careless kind of a civil nonsensical good-humour shandean book which will do all your heart good-- and all your head too provide you understand it
